(To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) By LINDA MARSA c.1995 Omni magazine (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  A major bank was about to approve a huge loan when the institution's officials called the Detective Information Network to do one last check on the applicant before they forked over nearly a million dollars.  They were glad they did. A little probing revealed their prospective client was a real miscreant who'd done prison time for convictions on 11 counts of fraud _ including lying on a loan application.  It was a close call for the greatly relieved bankers _ but all in a day's work for the network's investigators, who've been dubbed the ``Sam Spades of Cyberspace.''  Instead of a 38-caliber pistol and a nose for trouble, these cybersleuths are armed with PCs and modems which dial into nationwide databases.  These high-tech snoops can sniff out clues about the whereabouts of missing heirs, deadbeat dads and long-lost relatives.  They can unearth hidden property for those in the midst of a divorce or for creditors looking for assets in a bankruptcy, or find out if job applicants have inflated their resumes.  Best of all, they can ferret out this information without ever leaving their Washington, D.C.-area office.  ``We couldn't have done this 10 years ago _ the impact of technology is just incredible,'' says Charles Pinck, the network's research director, who believes the computerization of public information has completely changed the detective business.  An electronic wizard who loves to prowl through public records, Pinck's penchant for cloak-and-daggering seems to be genetic.  His father served under the legendary Wild Bill Donovan in World War II's OSS, forerunner of the CIA. Pinck himself honed his craft digging up dirt on political candidates.  ``No one is going to mistake me for a choirboy,'' he cheerfully admits.  Pinck started the network in 1994 with Francis Dillon, a retired general and former director of the Air Force Office of Special Investigations.  It seemed like a good combination of talent _ the military cop from the old school and the young turk who can deftly surf the Net.  ``We knew lots of people needed investigative advice from experienced pros but couldn't afford the services of a regular private eye,'' says Dillon, the network's managing director. ``So this fills that niche.''  They also tapped into the vast pool of retired investigators _ ex-informants, FBI and Secret Service agents _ in the nation's capital to do consultations with callers to their hot line.  ``We work with clients to narrow their search and figure out exactly what they're looking for,'' Dillon says.  Then for $30 to $40 an hour, the detectives scour cyberspace for any red flags such as bankruptcies, liens, judgments or problems with the IRS.  They scan databases to retrieve news items, voter registration and driving records. They can also look up information on prior court appearances, both criminal and civil.  The network, which claims an 80 percent success rate in locating missing people, has solved some intriguing cases by sifting through information on the infobahn.  A Texas man, for instance, was considering investing in a new airline. But before shelling out $10,000, he asked the network detectives to do a background check. They hit paydirt.  The airline's chairman had headed other dubious ventures and had been sanctioned by the Securities and Exchange Commission for selling $300,000 of unregistered stocks.  In another case _ these guys handle both commercial and domestic requests _ a woman had given her baby son to an American soldier in post-World War II Germany. She later married, had a family and emigrated to the United States. Now in failing health, she longed to be reunited with her son.  ``We got lucky because the Army officer had an unusual name,'' Dillon recalls. A national surname database that combines telephone directories and census records uncovered four possibilities. ``We let the family make the final calls,'' Dillon says.  ``People could execute many of these searches themselves,'' Pinck says. But they don't have the time or the patience. They don't know what to look for or where to look. Some are intimidated by the technology.  So for about $25, network staffers can tell you how to do it yourself. Sometimes a nudge in the right direction is all that is needed to jump-start a search.  While it may be true that new technologies and the accessibility of public databases increases opportunities for fraud and flimflam, if the Detective Information Network has its way, these same technologies will prove to be invaluable resources for professional and amateur detective alike. (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) By DEVERA PINE c.1995 Omni magazine (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  Take one helium balloon the size of the Empire State Building, attach a cabin 8 feet in diameter, throw in three crew members and lots of scientific equipment and send the whole thing 24 miles up and on a journey around the world.  The result? Odyssey, the first manned balloon expedition to circle the world while floating through the stratosphere.  If all goes well, the privately organized, all-volunteer expedition will launch in July 1996.  Although unmanned balloons have flown around the world in the stratosphere, Odyssey will be the first manned flight to do so _ and it will be the longest manned flight ever, lasting 18 days to 22 days.  In total, Odyssey will set three all-time records for manned balloon flights: altitude, distance and duration.  Moreover, Odyssey will be the first balloon _ manned or unmanned _ to fly around the Northern Hemisphere.  Until recently, politics prevented such flights. ``In the old days, getting shot at was certainly a consideration,'' says Robert Golden, director of the Particle Astrophysics Lab at New Mexico State University and Odyssey's volunteer chief engineer. (Golden died shortly before this article was published). ``You wouldn't have wanted to violate Soviet airspace with one of these things.''  With the end of the Cold War, Odyssey can now sail worry-free through the Northern Hemisphere _ though the crew still plans to avoid North Korea.  Flying at about 129,000 feet, Odyssey will qualify as a ``near-space'' balloon. The view from the windows of the gondola will look a lot like the view from a space-shuttle porthole _ the sky will be black, the stars may be out during the day and the blue band of the earth's atmosphere will be visible.  If the crew members have to make an emergency exit, they'll don pressure suits and portable oxygen tanks and free-fall to an altitude where they can open their parachutes _ and breathe. Needless to say, crew members Troy Bradley, Bob Martin and Mark Sullivan all have considerable experience parachuting.  More importantly from a scientific standpoint, Odyssey will fly above 99.9 percent of the earth's atmosphere, in the middle of the ozone layer. As a result, the expedition's organizers have consulted with scientists who want the balloon to carry experiments focusing on the ozone layer.  ``No one has flown (ozone experiments) that high for that long,'' says Kevin Roark, volunteer public-affairs director for the mission and a public-information specialist at Los Alamos National Lab.  Odyssey has yet another leg up on unmanned balloon flights when it comes to carrying out ozone research. Having a crew on board means the experiments don't have to be autonomous.  In between monitoring experiments, the crew will deal with living in the confines of the pressurized aluminum cabin.  In terms of people per cubic foot of space, the balloon has about as much room as the space shuttle, Golden says. But, he adds, ``the balloon won't have the advantage of zero gravity, which allows you to pack more people in.''  In addition to three crew members, Odyssey's gondola will house about 300 pounds of science instruments, batteries to power them as well as food and other supplies. The crew members will sleep in shifts in a bunk above the instrument rack. Odyssey's designers squeezed in a private toilet but no shower.  The similarities between Odyssey and the space shuttle haven't been lost on NASA, which plans to test the balloon's crew before, during and after the trip.  Like the crews of both long-duration shuttle flights and the not-yet-built space station, Odyssey's team must face cramped quarters, high altitudes and a demanding workload.  The information on crew interaction could prove invaluable to the agency as it examines the challenges involved in keeping astronauts in space for extended missions.  For instance, NASA will study how the crew works together in a potentially stressful and dangerous situation.  ``The gondola is 8 feet in diameter, you've got three people in it and you're flying an extremely complex aircraft. One mistake and you could have serious problems,'' says crew member Martin, a science reporter for KRQE-TV in New Mexico and a commercial pilot.  Another NASA experiment will monitor the crew's exposure to radiation. ``We know that people who fly on airliners get more radiation than people on the ground,'' Golden says. ``What's it going to be like for future hypersonic aircraft flying at the altitudes where our balloon is going to be?''  Other studies will track meteorological data, particularly information on thunder and lightning because the balloon is likely to fly over the top of thunderstorms.  Golden, who has sent experiments up in unmanned balloons, hopes that the mission will also improve the efficiency of balloon-piloting methods, opening the way for longer unmanned flights.  ``I have a 6,000-pound instrument that we fly about once a year for about a day, looking for antimatter,'' he says. ``We can't afford to fly it on a satellite. But a week on a balloon _ it's a poor man's satellite.''  Scientific concerns aside, however, the real force behind the mission lies in the quest for adventure. ``I always wanted to get on the shuttle,'' Martin says. ``This may be about as close as I'll ever get.''  (Devera Pine is a free-lance health and science writer who lives in New York City.) ------------------------------------------------- (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a color slide of Jason Priestley in a scene from ``Coldblooded.'' By MICHAEL SZYMANSKI Distributed by New York Times Special Features  Slapping his hockey stick to the ground, Jason Priestley smashes into comedian Mike Myers, steals the puck from Jason Hervey (``The Wonder Years'') and passes it to actor Peter Berg, who scores against goalie Andrew Shue (``Melrose Place'').  As the crowd in the stands roars, hockey great Wayne Gretsky high-fives Priestley, who then jeers at the stars on the opposing team.  The action is part of a charity game being played by members of the Hollywood All-Star Hockey League on a back lot at Universal Pictures' studios.  The star of the day is Priestley _ best known as Brandon Walsh on ``Beverly Hills 90210'' _ who is playing every bit as hard as the hockey pros on both teams.  ``In reality, I'm more competitive than I think Brandon would be,'' Priestley explains from the sidelines during a break in the action, ``but I'm certainly not as brutal as Cosmo. That guy's coldblooded.''  Cosmo is the character Priestley plays in the new movie ``Coldblooded,'' a black comedy that has opened in selected cities and will be released in others throughout September and October.  A few days later, when Priestley is out of his hockey togs and into his actor mode, he elaborates on the role that he _ and many critics _ think is his best to date: Cosmo, a mob hit man-in-training.  ``Cosmo is a blank canvas. He doesn't have hobbies like hockey,'' Priestley says during an interview in his agent's Hollywood office. ``He allows other people to paint colors on him, and even the people who Cosmo kills all color him one way or another and give the guy some sort of personality.''  The 26-year-old actor kicks his feet up on a desk and leans back on his chair, puffing on a Marlboro and rolling back the sleeves of his checked shirt. Dressed in pressed gray slacks and black shoes, with his hair in a high, flat-topped style, Priestly looks very much like his ``90210'' character. But unlike Brandon Walsh, the actor sports a slight mustache and beard.  ``I'm very proud of my work in this film, and it should help my movie career,'' says Priestley, whose feature credits include ``The Boy Who Could Fly'' (1986), ``Tombstone'' (1993), ``Nowhere to Run'' (1993) and ``Calendar Girl''(1993).  ``This is my best. People will be shocked.''  Priestley's ``Coldblooded'' character is a nerdy bookie for the mob who lives in the basement of a retirement center and leads a relatively boring life. His only distractions are his television set and occasional visits from his prostitute girlfriend Honey (Janeane Garofalo).  Things change dramatically when the mob boss (Robert Loggia) decides to turn Cosmo into a hit man and instructs veteran mobster Steve (Peter Riegert) to show Cosmo the ropes.  Riegert, who had never seen an episode of ``90210,'' says he was not sure what to expect from one of America's hottest teen idols.  ``Jason surprised me. He was terrific to work with,'' Riegert says during a separate interview. ``He knew how to underplay the role using great subtlety.''  As the film progresses, Cosmo realizes his new talent in gun marksmanship and becomes an accomplished, unflinching hit man. He also falls in love with a beautiful yoga instructor, played by Kimberly Williams, which complicates his life.  The movie was produced by Snowback Productions, the company headed by former ``Family Ties'' star Michael J. Fox.  In a cameo role, Fox plays one of Cosmo's victims. This allowed Priestley and Fox, who are off-screen friends, to work together for the first time.  ``I got a lot of satisfaction out of killing Michael J. Fox,'' Priestley says with a smile. ``We were doing the scene and I said, `Mike, how did we end up here like this, two kids from Vancouver where there are no guns?'  ``Here we are doing this movie and Michael is covered in blood. I think it was the first time he ever died on film and I was honored to do it.''  ``Coldblooded'' was written and directed by first-timer M. Wallace Wolodarsky. Shooting was completed in a little more than three weeks, with Priestley helping to make some changes in the script.  ``There's a line at the very beginning of the film that I added where a caller asks if I can make a suggestion (to the mob) that we take booking action on hockey games,'' says Priestley, who has played ice and street hockey since he was a child.  Hockey is one of the few hobbies Priestley still has time for as he juggles movie roles with his acting, directing and producing work on ``90210.''  The Canadian-born star says he is happy hockey is finally being taken seriously by Americans.  ``It got a bad rap because of all the fights going on during the games,'' Priestley says. ``But it's a different game now.''  Priestley enjoys both watching and playing hockey _ especially when he gets a chance to participate in games that help raise money for charities. Through the charity work, he has become friendly with hockey stars such as Gretsky, Brett Hull and Mark Messier.  ``It's fun because I get to hang out with my hockey idols and have a beer with them after the games,'' Priestley says. ``That's cool!''  In addition to his affinity for hockey, Priestley says he has an interest in guns _ which came in handy during his work on ``Coldblooded.'' Though the actor taped a gun-safety video for the National Rifle Association several years ago, his ``Coldblooded'' character routinely mishandles his weapon, even scratching his head with the barrel at one point.  ``This is not a gun-safety movie,'' says Priestley, who has several guns of his own. ``It's a gun comedy.''  Guns played a big part in the final all-night shoot for ``Coldblooded,'' which took place at a firing range. Director Wolodarsky, Riegert and Priestley watched the sun go down, filmed their scenes and watched the sun come up.  ``It was a surreal night, real spooky _ and it comes across on film. We were shooting real rounds and I'm pretty good with a gun,'' Priestley says. ``I'm absolutely comfortable with a gun. But could I actually take a human life? I don't know.''  Lack of a killer instinct is one way Priestley differs from his film character. The two also have distinct temperaments.  ``I'm not as calm as Cosmo. I'm a more of a kinetic kind of person,'' Priestley says. ``There was this guy in high school I knew who would walk around alone and talk to rocks and stuff, and he reminds me of Cosmo.  ``I'm a bit offbeat myself in real life, but not that bad. Yet I'm not really as straight-laced as Brandon either.''  Fans may be surprised to learn that Priestley sometimes participates in chat groups on the Internet, using the sign-on of a friend.  ``I'm amazed at how much people gossip about meeting me in places I've never been and all kinds of other stuff,'' he says. ``I'm really just a regular guy.''  Though he hates the ``icon'' label that his hit TV show has brought him, Priestley says he expects to stay with ``90210'' for several more years.  ``Will our show be like the `Brady Bunch' in the year 2010? I wonder about things like that,'' Priestley says. ``It's kind of weird to be a part of that.  ``Will they put the Walsh kitchen in the Smithsonian museum? Will there be a reunion show? Will they do a movie with a whole new cast? Will some of us never work again?''  He's doing everything he can to prevent that and says he wouldn't mind making another quirky picture like ``Coldblooded.''  ``This is the first time a film turned out as I imagined it would,'' Priestley says. ``I laughed out loud when I read it, it was so twisted.''  While he has certainly been successful thus far, Priestley concedes he may never achieve his goal of becoming the modern-day Cary Grant.  ``I've been a huge Cary Grant fan all my life,'' the actor says. ``When I was growing up I aspired to be like him.  ``Unfortunately, it looks like Hugh Grant filled those shoes. God, he's a charming guy, isn't he? I hate him.'' c.1995 Michael Szymanski  (Michael Szymanski is a Hollywood-based free-lance writer.) ------------------------------------------------- (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a color slide of Linda Fiorentino in a scene from ``Jade.'' By CINDY PEARLMAN Distributed by New York Times Special Features  A reporter walks up to Linda Fiorentino in the lobby of New York City's Regency Hotel and proceeds to give her a line.  ``So, Linda,'' he says in a Texas drawl. ``I hear that your new movie is a lot like `Basic Instinct.'''  Apparently, this comparison doesn't sit too well with the actress.  ``Let's get this straight,'' she says bitingly. ``I am no Sharon Stone.''  Undaunted, the man presses on.  ``I know 10 guys who would leapfrog over Sharon Stone to get to you,'' he says.  Fiorentino doesn't crack a smile. Cool, calm and collected, she reaches into her purse and takes out a notebook.  ``Here's a piece of paper,'' she says with an icy stare. ``List these men. Don't forget the phone numbers.''  One thing is clear from this encounter: Fiorentino is one tough cookie.  And that's just the kind of woman she plays in the new movie ``Jade,'' which will open nationwide Oct. 13.  Fiorentino stars as Trina Gavin, a psychologist by day who is a sexy adulteress by night. Upset over her hubby's infidelities, she decides to show him that two can play that game.  Like Stone's character in ``Basic Instinct'' (1992), Gavin may also have the nasty habit of killing the men she takes to bed.  David Caruso co-stars as an assistant district attorney who investigates a murder in which Trina may be a suspect. He also happens to be Trina's college boyfriend.  Fiorentino has come to the Regency today to promote ``Jade,'' which was written by Joe Eszterhas and directed by William Friedkin.  Dressed in a black stretch catsuit, she stalks her way into one of the hotel's suite and begins sizing up the journalists who have come to interview her.  The men in the room seem to be easy targets for the 35-year-old actress's rapier wit.  A male reporter starts out by telling Fiorentino that her ``Jade'' co-star Michael Biehn (who plays a homicide detective) recently received some sexy nighties from a fan through the mail.  ``What's the weirdest gift a fan ever sent you?'' he asks.  ``Michael got the lingerie! I was hoping it got to him,'' Fiorentino says with a husky laugh.  Actually, lingerie is something with which Fiorentino became quite familiar during the many explicit sex scenes in ``Jade.''  During an earlier interview, Chazz Palminteri (who plays Gavin's cheating husband) said that the love scenes he shared with Fiorentino made him a bit uncomfortable.  ``Linda had a certain confidence,'' the actor said. ``We'd get on the set and _ whoosh! _ her clothes were off. I was nervous. I haven't done many love scenes.''  When Fiorentino hears this her eyes light up.  ``Had I known that Chazz had never done a sex scene before I would have been more gentle with him,'' she says. ``I always am with any virgin I encounter.''  Then, on a more serious note, the actress admits that those erotic scenes weren't really easy for her, either.  ``Look, I come from a family of eight kids,'' she says. ``We were a good Catholic family, which means I think my mother had sex eight times.''  Another male journalist decides to pounce on this statement.  ``If your family was so uptight,'' he says, ``how did you become such an exhibitionist in this movie?''  Fiorentino tosses him a long, penetrating stare that could stop a clock. He squirms.  ``I don't understand,'' she says finally. ``Are you asking me if I run around naked all the time?''  The reporter blushes and begins to stammers. ``No, no. I didn't _ uh, I'm sorry.''  Satisfied with this reaction, Fiorentino decides to answer her own question.  ``Yes, I did run around naked all the time,'' she says. ``When I was 6 years old. I guess that's how I got the guts for `Jade.'''  A few moments later, she reveals how her own attitudes toward sex differ from those of her character.  ``I'm not really sure if I am free about sex,'' she says. ``I look at the things I do in `Jade' and have to justify it by saying it's not me. It's just me playing a role. It's a movie.  ``It's part of the job to pretend that you're not inhibited when you're doing a sex scene. After the fact, I freaked out. At the end of the day on `Jade' I was like, `Oh, my God! I did that?'  ``But it's just part of the job.''  And it's a job that Fiorentino almost passed on.  ``I was skeptical going in,'' she says. ``I didn't know if I could do this part because it was representing a man's idea of what a woman is. The fantasy seemed more male than female.  ``I decided her extracurricular activities were an extreme reaction to finding out her husband was unfaithful. Wealth and power replaced sex in that relationship. And she wanted the power.''  Trina is not the first powerful woman Fiorentino has portrayed. She garnered a great deal of publicity for her role as femme fatale Bridget Gregory in last year's ``The Last Seduction.''  ``When I read the script I just thought, `This is my part. No one else will get it.' I walked into director John Dahl's office and made that very clear. I said, `John, no one else plays this part but me.'''  Gregory is a woman who steals her husband's hot cash, then moves to a small town where she becomes a ruthless, murderous vixen.  ``It was a therapeutic role,'' Fiorentino says. ``Sure, this woman was not exactly a role model for little girls, but she was smart. She was a professional. She was unique.''  It was also a role Fiorentino knew could have a big impact on her career.  ``I thought if anybody ever saw the film, it would either change my life or ruin it,'' she says. ``If it didn't touch a nerve in people, then I don't know what would have happened. It was a real gamble.''  The gamble paid off with a Best Actress award from the New York Film Critics Circle and a near nomination for an Academy Award. The latter was canceled after ``Seduction'' was declared ineligible for Academy Award consideration because it had premiered on cable television.  Even without an Oscar, Fiorentino is on her way to becoming a true Hollywood star.  But it's the work that interests her, she says, not the fame. She doesn't read Hollywood power lists and she doesn't really care who's hot and who's not.  ``Does anyone really care?'' she asks with a sigh.  ``Some people do,'' a male journalist suggests.  Fiorentino flicks him off like a fly. ``They do? I don't know anybody who cares if they're hot. But maybe that's because I keep good company.''  That said, she won't reveal much about the company she keeps. This is one actress who wants to keep her private life private.  The one thing she will say is that she grew up in a family of eight children in South Philadelphia.  After graduating from Rosemont College with a degree in political science, she was thinking about law school. Instead, on the advice of a teacher, she moved to New York to study at the Circle in the Square Theater.  Her first film work came a few years later when she landed a role in the high school wrestling melodrama ``Vision Quest'' (1985).  ``The whole movie made me miserable, but I was just out of school,'' she says. ``What could I do? I didn't have a million choices.''  Afterward, she showed up in Martin Scorsese's ``After Hours'' (1985), playing a kinky sculptress, and Alan Rudolph's ``The Moderns'' (1988). A small role as Joe Mantegna's very upset wife in ``Queens Logic'' followed in 1991.  Fiorentino met Dahl when she tested for the role that went to Lara Flynn Boyle in the director's 1993 film ``Red Rock West.'' He remembered her later on, when he was casting the role of Bridget in ``The Last Seduction.''  Fiorentino has teamed with Dahl again for ``Unforgettable'' in which she portrays a scientist who discovers a memory drug. That film will be released later this year or early in 1996.  Though the actress hasn't chosen a follow-up role as yet, she's been told that Quentin Tarantino is dying to work with her.  ``That would be a riot,'' she says, laughing.  Since she seems to be in a good mood at the moment, another male journalist decides to step up to bat.  ``Could you have handled this success 10 years ago?'' he asks.  ``I can't predict what would have happened,'' Fiorentino replies. ``I suspect because I have 10 years of abuse behind me that I handle success a little differently. I think I'm more aloof than I otherwise would be.''  Another male journalist pipes up and asks: ``What constitutes abuse?''  Fiorentino shoots him daggers. ``What constitutes abuse for women in Hollywood?'' she snaps. ``Use your imagination, pal.''  Sharon Stone would have been proud. c.1995 Cindy Pearlman  (Cindy Pearlman is a Chicago-based free-lance writer.) ------------------------------------------------- (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950925.0209 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-SOUTH-POLE-INFO-PAGEUP-NYTSF) </DATELINE> <TEXT> 25 FASCINATING FACTS ABOUT ANTARCTICA (This ``separate buy'' article is from this week's PageUp Lifestyle and Entertainment Package.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) By ROXANNE NELSON c.1995 Hemispheres magazine (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  1. Despite its snowy appearance, Antarctica is the driest place on Earth. Some areas receive less than 2 inches of rain a year.  2. Antarctica is the only continent without an indigenous land mammal population. Polar bears, caribou and Eskimos are found north in the Arctic.  3. Tourism to Antarctica is still in its infant stage and may well remain so because of climatic and environmental considerations. Only about 80,000 people have set foot in Antarctica.  4. Visitors coming ashore on Antarctica's coastline will be greeted by an overpowering aroma _ penguin poop, or guano, as it is known in intimate circles.  5. Antarctica has no hotels or anything remotely resembling tourist lodging. Your cruise ship is your home for the duration of a visit, unless you are invited to stay at a research station.  6. The coldest temperature on Earth was reported at the Vostok Base, located 1,200 kilometers inland (746 miles): a frosty -128.6 degrees Fahrenheit (-89.2 Celsius).  7. Travel to Antarctica is limited to the austral summer, roughly the period of December to February. Otherwise, the weather is too treacherous and unpredictable to safely make the journey, to say nothing of the extremely short winter days and the possibility of your ship becoming ice-locked when the waters surrounding the continent freeze.  8. There are bubbling, hot, volcanic springs at Deception Island. If you are brave enough to remove your parka and strip to a bathing suit, you can enjoy nature's natural Jacuzzi.  9. Antarctica is the highest place on Earth, with more than half of the continent measuring 2,000 meters above sea level (6,562 feet). The highest point is Mount Vinson Massif, measuring 5,140 meters high (16,864 feet). Adventure-travel companies offer climbing treks up Mount Vinson.  10. The only human civilizations are the scientific research stations. Some, such as Chilean Marsh Station and American McMurdo, are quite large and resemble towns.  11. Likewise, there are no shopping malls, freeways, traffic jams, inner cities or coffeehouses. It is the only place on Earth that is truly quiet, more silent than even the desert.  12. Antarctica wasn't successfully traversed by land until 1957-1958 when an English team including Sir Edmund Hillary (of Mount Everest fame) crossed the continent. The previous attempt had been in 1914, when Sir Ernest Shackleton's ship froze in the ice and was crushed.  13. The first explorer to reach the South Pole, Roald Amundsen, hadn't planned on going south. His lifelong dream was to be the first to reach the North Pole, but when news came that it had been conquered by Robert Peary, he decided to try for the South Pole.  14. The ice cap covering Antarctica is about two miles thick in some areas, developing over millions of years as scant layers of snowfall accumulated and never melted.  15. Antarctica is frozen today, but evidence shows that it was once covered with trees and sustained many forms of plant and mammal life.  16. Gondwana is the name of the ancient supercontinent that is believed to have consisted of Africa, Antarctica, Australia, India, New Zealand and South America. Fossilized leaves and bird skeletons found in Antarctica and its surrounding islands match species found in both Australia and South America.  17. The hole in the ozone layer was first discovered in Antarctica by British scientists at their base in Halley Bay.  18. Antarctica is rich in sea life. Six species of seals, nine species of whales and seven species of penguins call Antarctica home for at least part of the year.  Among numerous other sea birds is the extraordinary Arctic tern, which migrates between the Arctic and the Antarctic _ a total of 25,000 miles per year. The surrounding seas harbor many species of fish and other sea life.  19. Tiny lichen and other minute forms of plant life grow in parts of Antarctica, dispelling the myth that the continent is lifeless.  20. The dry valleys, ice-free zones, more closely resemble alien worlds than anywhere else on Earth. NASA carried out much of its work there before sending the Viking probe to Mars.  21. The Antarctic environment is extremely fragile, its ecosystem unable to tolerate any disruptions. A footprint in a rain forest might disappear after half an hour, but in the Antarctic, it might take 10 years.  Tourists must strictly adhere to the guidelines and rules given them before going ashore. It can sometimes be literally a matter of life or death. Storms can blow up without warning, and evacuation must take place with utmost speed.  22. Traces of pesticides have been found in the ice, which gives scientists a good idea how widespread the pollution of our planet is.  23. Antarctica belongs to no single nation, although several nations have laid claim to the continent based on discoveries by early explorers. The Antarctic Treaty of 1961 did not actually settle the disputes, but merely shelved them. Its purpose was to ensure international cooperation.  24. Greenpeace and other conservation groups would like to make Antarctica a ``world park'' to be used for peaceful purposes only, ensuring that all wildlife will be protected and the continent will never be exploited. So far the first step in that goal has been the attainment of a 50-year moratorium on mineral and oil exploration.  25. Antarctica is simply, without question, the most beautiful place on Earth and one that still remains unconquered.  (Roxanne Nelson is a California-based free-lance writer and world traveler.) ------------------------------------------------- (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) By ANN E. LaFORGE c.1995 Child magazine (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  Most parents want to raise kids who will contribute to the good of the world. But these days, it's hard to know what values to teach and how to convey them most effectively. Everybody from politicians to preachers seem to have a different idea of right and wrong.  ``Many parents are insecure or uncertain about how to teach values to their children,'' says Richard Eyre, co-author of ``Teaching Your Children Values'' (Simon &AMP; Schuster, 1993). But, he adds, ``Who would you rather have influencing your child _ you or MTV?''  Even if you don't have all the answers, you can still raise a moral child. In fact, children usually begin to learn values from the little things you do, like hugging them when they do well and teaching them to say ``Thank you'' even when they don't want to.  ``Kids learn right from wrong mainly by observing the actions of their parents,'' says Michael Schulman, co-author of ``Bringing Up a Moral Child'' (Addison-Wesley, 1985)  Accordingly, here are 40 ways to raise a caring kid. -0-  1. The magic word. Teach your children ``magic'' words like ``thank you,'' ``please'' and ``excuse me.'' Whenever they use one, act like you're under their spell and quickly grant all polite requests, as long as they're reasonable.  2. Tea for two. Have a dress-up tea party with your children. Ask them to invite their favorite stuffed animals. Then, try to outdo each other's politeness.  3. Lend a hug. Give a special hug or a pat on the head or shoulder when your children are being nice to someone or simply behaving.  4. Rock the vote. Take your children along with you when you vote, and explain why it's important to cast your ballot at election time.  5. Honesty policy. Always answer your child's questions truthfully.  6. Praise others. When you and your children encounter helpful or polite people, make note of their kindness.  7. Pillow talk. Every night, when you tuck your children in, ask them, ``What was the best part of the day?'' and ``What was the hardest part of the day?'' Then zip your lips and listen to what's on their minds without judging, lecturing or interrupting.  8. The if ... then game. If someone you don't often see is about to visit your home, play an if ... then game to help your children make guests feel welcome. You present the if part _ ``If Susan likes crayons ... '' _ and have your children supply the ``then'' _ `` ... then we can color.''  9. Sort and save. Have your children put cans, bottles and newspapers in recycling bins. Be environmentally creative, and encourage them to find new uses for old things.  10. Be kind, rewind. Make sure your children see you rewinding videotapes, and explain why it's considerate.  11. Set goals. Help your children work toward realistic objectives such as saving money for a special toy or reading a certain number of books.  12. Give a hint. When someone says ``Hello'' to your children or compliments them and they don't respond, help them find the polite words _ ``Come say `Hello' to Ms. Jones, Ann.''  13. It's cool to be kind. Acknowledge any acts of kindness your children initiate _ ``It was nice of you to help your sister.'' Be sure to praise your children, as well as the action.  14. Fight fair. When you and your spouse disagree, avoid making hostile, accusatory remarks, casting blame or and calling each other names. Find a way to compromise and make up.  15. Vent creatively. When you're angry, do something you'd like your children to do. For example, draw a picture of how you feel or rip up some old newspapers instead of lashing out.  16. Hands off. Establish a no-hurting policy at home. Send any overly aggressive child to a designated ``cool-down'' spot.  17. A friend in deed. Take your children with you to visit and help care for people who are less fortunate. Explain how good it makes you feel to you help others.  18. Ask first. Always ask permission before taking, using or throwing away something that belongs to your children.  19. Movie mania. Plan to watch an inspiring videotape such as ``It's a Wonderful Life'' or ``Beauty and the Beast.'' Make popcorn.  20. Adjective action. Have a contest to see how many adjectives the family can think up to describe how people feel. Set a goal of 50 words such as ``joyful,'' ``cranky'' and ``hopeful,'' and try to meet it. Then post the list and keep adding to it.  21. Talk it out. If another child starts crying on the playground, talk about it. Ask your children, ``How do you think that little boy is feeling right now? What do you think made him unhappy?''  22. Local heroes. Talk about people in your community who are doing good deeds. Read aloud heartwarming newspaper stories.  23. If it doesn't belong to you ...  If your child steals a toy from school, explain why it's wrong to keep it and have him/her give it back.  24. Role reversal. Give your children a chance to see how it feels to be in someone else's shoes: For 10 or 15 minutes, let them pretend to be the parent, while you pretend to be a child.  25. New kid in town. When someone new moves into your neighborhood, pay a ``welcome'' visit with your children and bring along a card or some cookies.  26. Be a clean team. If you and your children notice litter while taking a walk, pick up the garbage and put it in the trash.  27. Second chances. If your children lie about some household episode, contain your anger and remind them how important it is to tell the truth. Then give them a second chance. When they come clean, let your praise outweigh the punishment.  28. Cause and effect. When practical, let your children feel the consequences of their mistakes _ ``If you leave your bike out in the rain, it gets rusty.''  29. Dollars and sense. Help your children learn to manage money by giving them a weekly allowance and letting them decide how to spend it.  30. Do the right thing. Admit to your children that it's sometimes hard to be honest. Praise them when they resist a strong temptation to cheat or lie.  31. Don't send mixed messages. Never ask your child to lie for you _ ``Tell her I'm not here'' or ``Don't tell your dad I bought this.''  32. Thanks, I guess. Teach your children to say ``Thank you'' even for gifts they don't love them. Pretend to give them some funny presents, then ask them to practice saying ``Thank you.''  33. Never break a promise. Don't promise your child a punishment or reward that you cannot, will not or do not deliver _ ``If you don't clean up your room right now, you'll never have another friend over again.''  34. Baby love. Ask older children to help you care for your baby. Have them suggest ways to soothe a crying baby.  35. Bench warmers. Designate a chair as a ``fighting bench'' in your home. When two kids fight, send them to the ``bench,'' where they must stay seated until each child can tell you what he/she did wrong.  36. Mix It Up. join a play group, or find an extracurricular activity for your child that includes people from different racial, religious or economic backgrounds.  37. A ``Hands-off'' box. To reduce battles over toys, have each child decorate a personal toy box. Inside it, keep all the toys that they'd rather not share with siblings or friends who come over to play. Make sure you leave some toys out for all the children to share.  38. Your lips are sealed. When your children face difficult moral dilemmas, such as peer pressure to cheat or steal, resist the urge to supply all the answers. Instead, use role play to help them think through their feelings and find their own solutions.  39. Television talk. When you watch TV or videos, talk about the characters' actions and their effect on others _ ``If that character hadn't lied, he wouldn't be in so much trouble now'' or ``It took a lot of courage for her to tell the truth, but it was worth it in the end.''  40. Fan mail. When your child gives you a special present, send him/her a thank-you note through the mail.  (Ann E. LaForge is a Durham, N.C.-based free-lance writer.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a 350-word sidebar By JOANN GRECO c.1995 A&AMP;E Monthly (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  Sit back and imagine your dream house. Everybody has one. Maybe a lush, tropical paradise, sporting a kidney-shaped, mosaic-rimmed pool.  Or an ultramodern mansion, with a media center to house a 50-inch projection television and Bang and Olufsen silver-sleek stereo components?  How about ye olde country estate, where a canopied bed beckons, piled with a glossy magazine's worth of lace-edged pillows and the most sumptuous of downy white comforters?  If given the choice, I'd settle for more closets.  But others _ the truly rich _ dream a little bigger, and for them dreams very often come true.  A century ago families such as the Astors, Hearsts and Vanderbilts tossed their pennies into the air and, voila, the magnificent, European-inspired, turn-of-the-century mansions emerged.  Today, when you peek inside the multimillion-dollar, custom-built homes of the monied, you may notice some differences.  ``Compared to when the rich were trying to emulate the classic villas of Italy or the palazzos of Renaissance Europe, today's wealthy tend to incorporate the latest technology and to look for intelligent buildings,'' says Raj Barr-Kumar, past vice president of the American Institute of Architects.  ``This kind of home is always going to be a statement that you've arrived,'' he says, ``but the way you create comfort and express yourself today is through technological innovations. There aren't so many attempts to recreate Windsor Castle.''  Well, actually, there is the guy in San Jose, Calif., who's trying to unload his 23-room replica of that fortress for $3.5 million. And spiral staircases and marble foyers do still abound. But traditional touches of luxury aside, many megamansions excel in over-the-top indulgences.  Before we get to the extreme, let's begin with the sublime. Let's take a walk in the remote woods of Mill Run, Penn.  Here the modern era of America's castles begins, with Frank Lloyd Wright's Fallingwater, arguably the most famous residence in America other than the White House. Eye-catching, sure, but you'll find no exterior marble facings here, no gilt-edged furnishings, no crystal chandeliers.  Instead, Fallingwater represents the epitome of Wright's philosophy of organic architecture, his ideal of lessening the barriers between nature and structure. The house straddles a stream and is rooted to a rock on top of the property's natural waterfall.  Owner Edgar Kaufmann expected his vacation home to take advantage of the views. He was unprepared for the master builder's response: ``I want you to live with the waterfall, not just to look at it.''  Despite the appearance of its massive exterior and soaring interiors, the 1936 building occupies hardly more than 5,000 square feet, nothing compared to some other modern dream homes.  Yet, the building is awe-inspiring. ``Fallingwater just bowls you over,'' says Joan Wilson, a curator who has worked there for 25 years. ``It's got everything going for it _ gorgeous site, original Wright furniture, original art collection. It's so unusual in every way.'' It cost only $155,000 to build _ and even that was way over budget.  Western Pennsylvania is an unlikely setting for a dream house. In general, the most gorgeous homes are in Colorado, California, Florida or Hawaii.  The Hawaiian estate created by hotelier Christopher Hernmeter may be the pinnacle of uberresidences. This 30,000-square-foot edifice opens onto the corals, deep purples and emerald greens of its setting via a one-ton bronze door that alone cost a cool $1 million.  Through the baronial entrance await Parisian and Florentine antiques, a ``his'' bathroom styled to resemble the burled-walnut richness of an English haberdashery, a 10,000-square-foot master suite, travertine-marble fireplaces, muraled and coffered ceilings, etched glass screens and a roomful of gadgetry for controlling the climate and manipulating the switchless lights and invisible telephones.  ``Hernmeter wanted to create something people would speak of and remember,'' says designer Randy Patton, who helped complete the interiors. Patton says the ultraformal elegance of the home is atypical for Hawaii. ``He wasn't interested in something that evoked bikinis and luaus.''  Waterfront footage means a lot on the West Coast, and architect Robert Earl is particularly proud of his Newport Beach, Calif., masterpiece _ an 18,000-square-foot edifice with a limestone facade and Versailles-style furnishings.  Earl believes that, technology aside, today's castles still ``seek to re-create the flavor of European country homes.'' From the outside, the home looks like a row of Italianate town houses to be found lining a London park.  Inside, the $13 million mansion is a marvel of creamy gentility, with ivory-colored marble flooring, butter-yellow-and-taupe upholstery, gilt-edged furniture, a skylit rotunda in one of the 13 bathrooms and seemingly acres of eggshell-toned draperies.  Louis XIV chairs and French fripperies just wouldn't work at North Star Lodge, a custom-built log home near Aspen, Colo. Still, Honest Abe would hardly recognize this gleaming, gloriously grand ``cabin,'' which serves as a second home for an international lawyer.  Some 550 logs of standing dead spruce and pine add up to a 16,000-square-foot home where everything _ from the imposing dining-room chairs to the rough-hewn staircase _ is made of logs, twigs and branches.  This tour ends in a forest outside Seattle, where software developer Bill Gates _ America's richest man _ is building a 42,000-square-foot estate for $35 million. In a nod to eco-conscious northwesterners, much of the lumber is from old buildings and, upon completion, an extensive reforestation effort will be undertaken.  As befits Gates' hermitlike reputation, the main house will be burrowed into the sloping site, camouflaged with wood, glass and rock, and hidden behind trees.  It's what is inside that is really making architects, technology-freaks and celebrity-watchers take note.  Walls will be hung with flat-panel, high-definition screens, the better to showcase the 100,000 electronic images of works of art Gates owns. As the mogul strolls from room to room, the image he's selected will follow him, or merge effortlessly into the background.  Throughout the house, state-of-the-art and beyond-the-art devices will control electrical and mechanical systems.  Art that moves with you, switchless lamps, invisible telephones. Marble, crystal and gold. Sweeping staircases and sweeping vistas. It's all at your fingertips _ if you've got the money to pay. -0- SIDEBAR: WHAT DO SIMPLE FOLK DO?  Now, let's be realistic. What comforts can the average homeowner expect?  First of all, experts caution, there's no such thing as ``average,'' thanks to variations in location, size and budget.  But according to the National Association of Home Builders and the U.S. Census Bureau in 1993, Mr. and Mrs. Joe Median built a typical new home that was 1,945 square feet at a cost of about $126,000.  Only 13 percent of homes hover near the more-than-3,000-square-foot mark _ and only 9 percent of homes top $250,000. A million or so new homes are built each year _ a minuscule addition to the huge pool of 110 million existing homes.  Of those new homes, more than a third are custom-built, meaning owners turn to stock plans or general contractors rather than selecting an already-built house in a new, speculative development. A 1,600-square-foot home can be bought for $80,000, exclusive of land.  But if you want to approach the range of the supercastles, you need to hire an architect. Fewer than 5 percent of homeowners do that, estimates Raj Barr-Kumar, an architect in Washington, D.C.  ``I'd say the upper bracket begins at a 6,000-square-foot or 7,000-square-foot home,'' he says, before reeling off the standard accoutrements for such a home: ``four-car garage, double-height living room, a grand staircase, preferably in two big swoops, `his' and `her' bathrooms.'' That would place a home in the $1 million range.  Throw in a spa, heated indoor pool, bowling alley and 25-seat theater and we're talking about 10,000 square feet and $3 million. ``An elevator always gives extra cachet,'' he adds, ``and it doesn't even cost a lot, about $25,000.''  ``But,'' Barr-Kumar chuckles, ``designing for the rich isn't necessarily easy. There's always never enough money. Most people want more than their budget can cover _ I'm sure even Bill Gates' budget won't be enough.''  (JoAnn Greco is a Philadelphia-based writer specializing in business, the arts and travel.) ------------------------------------------------- (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) (Bill Cunningham/New York Times Photo)  (NYT21) WASHINGTON -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- EXCLUSIVE-GINGRICH, 1-09 -- A tape of a telephone call between Speaker Newt Gingrich - shown greeting House members Thursday - and other Republicans shows him helping to orchestrate a response to charges by an ethics subcommittee. The call was taped by people in Florida who were unsympathetic to Gingrich and who heard it on a police scanner that picked up the cell phone transmissions. It was given to a Democratic congressman, who made the tape available to The New York Times. (Amy Toensing/New York Times Photo)  (NYT20) VARNER, Ark. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- ARK-EXECUTE-CLR, 1-09 -- Virginia Hamilton and David Small hug each other after Paul Ruiz and Earl Van Denton are executed in Arkansas on Wednesday. The two men shot Small and killed Mrs. Hamilton's father and six others in 1977. ``I hope I have some relief and the nightmares will stop,'' says Anne Jester, whose father, Opal James, a 58-year-old park ranger, was also killed by Van Denton and Ruiz after their escape from prison in Oklahoma. (Spencer Tirey/New York Times Photo)  (NYT19) JACKSONVILLE, Fla. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- JAGUARS-OWNER-CLR, 1-09 -- Wayne Weaver, who once guided the growth of the Nine West shoe retailer, is leading the expansion Jacksonville Jaguars to success in the NFL. Weaver insists that the rules for operating an expansion football team are similar to those for running other companies: know your product and your consumer, hire smart people, and give them authority and support. (Oscar Sosa/New York Times Photo)  (NYT18) NANG RONG, Thailand -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- THAI-ENTREPENEUR, 1-09 -- Employees of the shoe factory in rural Nang Rong are just as busy as their counterparts in Bangkok, but outside work they have been able to recapture a more measured way of life. The factory here is one of several such enterprises created by Mechai Viravaidya, who has turned his energies toward the problem of rural poverty. ``It's reverse migration,'' he says. ``We have one factory where 80 percent of the people have come back from Bangkok.'' (Seth Mydans/New York Times Photo)  (NYT17) BOSTON -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- PETS-DAMAGE-BOS-CLR, 1-9 -- ``I have trained my dogs to respect where they can and cannot sleep,'' says Tom Myers, relaxing with his dogs, Rupert, Zahn and Maxwell. ``When I'm gone during the day, the dogs are in their crates where they feel secure. In the evenings when I'm home they're allowed on one sofa, which is covered in a linen fabric. Untrained dogs can tear a house apart just like spoiled children.'' (Pam Berry/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT16) SUDBURY, Mass. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- CHILDCARE-COLUMN-BOS-CLR, 1-9 -- Rich and Dotty Robison pose with their children: from left to right, Jason, 12, Christina, 16, and Amy, 14. Both Jason and Amy have Down's Syndrome. ``This is not like getting a boy instead of a girl, it's not just that this baby isn't what you expected,'' Rich says. ``It's your future. Any ideas you had about it don't count now.'' (Christopher Fitzgerald/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT15) QUINCY, Mass. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- TANGO-MAN-BOS-CLR, 1-9 --Fernando Arredondo holds a bandoneon, an instrument used exclusively for the tango. Arredondo has one of the biggest collections of tango music in the world, including 4,000 tango records that he refuses to sell, even though they are valued at $25,000. ``Because Carlos Gardel died in Colombia, we Colombians love tango just as much as the Argentinians,'' he says of tango's biggest star, the Argentinian crooner who died in a plane crash in 1935. (Barry Chin/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT14) CAPE COD, Mass. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- CANCER-BREAST-II-2-CLR, 1-09 -- Cheryl Osimo, who developed breast cancer in 1991 though she had no family history of the disease, with her daughter Cara, 11. ``My life changed, and I watched my children suffer as they watched me endure what happened,'' says Ms. Osimo, who runs a day care center and is a member of the Massachusetts Breast Cancer Coalition. ``It was the most fearful experience I've had in my entire life.'' (Pam Berry/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT13) UNDATED -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- CANCER-BREAST-II-1-CLR, 1-09 -- Suzanne Ballard recently underwent a masectomy and is using holistic methods to battle her breast cancer, which is in the advanced stages. ``People have mainly tended to think of breast cancer as a lifestyle disease, and not a disease of chemicals or environmental or occupational exposures,'' says Robert Hoover, an epidemiologist with the National Cancer Institute. (Pam Berry/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT12) SALEM, Mass. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- CANCER-BREAST-2-CLR, 1-09 -- Rev. (Pam Berry/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT16) SUDBURY, Mass. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- CHILDCARE-COLUMN-BOS-CLR, 1-9 -- Rich and Dotty Robison pose with their children: from left to right, Jason, 12, Christina, 16, and Amy, 14. Both Jason and Amy have Down's Syndrome. ``This is not like getting a boy instead of a girl, it's not just that this baby isn't what you expected,'' Rich says. ``It's your future. Any ideas you had about it don't count now.'' (Christopher Fitzgerald/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT15) QUINCY, Mass. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- TANGO-MAN-BOS-CLR, 1-9 --Fernando Arredondo holds a bandoneon, an instrument used exclusively for the tango. Arredondo has one of the biggest collections of tango music in the world, including 4,000 tango records that he refuses to sell, even though they are valued at $25,000. ``Because Carlos Gardel died in Colombia, we Colombians love tango just as much as the Argentinians,'' he says of tango's biggest star, the Argentinian crooner who died in a plane crash in 1935. (Barry Chin/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT14) CAPE COD, Mass. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- CANCER-BREAST-II-2-CLR, 1-09 -- Cheryl Osimo, who developed breast cancer in 1991 though she had no family history of the disease, with her daughter Cara, 11. ``My life changed, and I watched my children suffer as they watched me endure what happened,'' says Ms. Osimo, who runs a day care center and is a member of the Massachusetts Breast Cancer Coalition. ``It was the most fearful experience I've had in my entire life.'' (Pam Berry/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT13) UNDATED -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- CANCER-BREAST-II-1-CLR, 1-09 -- Suzanne Ballard recently underwent a masectomy and is using holistic methods to battle her breast cancer, which is in the advanced stages. ``People have mainly tended to think of breast cancer as a lifestyle disease, and not a disease of chemicals or environmental or occupational exposures,'' says Robert Hoover, an epidemiologist with the National Cancer Institute. (Pam Berry/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT12) SALEM, Mass. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- CANCER-BREAST-2-CLR, 1-09 -- Rev. Elizabeth Ann King, 35, a minister the Tabernacle Congregational Church in Salem, was diagnosed with breast cancer last year. ``I said, 'You've got to be kidding!' `` recalls Ms. King. ``The disease is just as random as a tornado dropping out of the sky and ripping your life apart.'' In 1997 about 186,000 American women will be told that they, too, have breast cancer. (Tom Herde/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT11) WALTHAM, Mass. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- CANCER-BREAST-1-CLR, 1-09 -- ``With all the chemicals having been introduced since World War II, there's a great deal about which to be suspicious,'' says Joan Berns, who was diagnosed with breast cancer in 1992 at age 43, and who co-wrote a handbook on how to deal with the disease. In 1997 about 186,000 American women will be told that they, too, have breast cancer. (Pam Berry/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT10) NEW YORK -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- GREENWICH-VILLAGE-2-CLR, 1-09 -- A Greenwich Village landmark: The Jefferson Market Courthouse, now a neighborhood library, viewed from the west on West 10th Street. Greenwich Village is bordered on the east by Broadway and on the west by the Hudson River; on the south by the north side of Houston Street and on the north by the south side of 14th Street. (Sara Krulwich/New York Times Photo)  (NYT9) NEW YORK -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- GREENWICH-VILLAGE-1-CLR, 1-09 -- The Washington Arch frames Henry James-era brownstones, now owned by New York University, along the north side of Washington Square Park. Though still the setting for Shakespeare, jazz concerts and chess players, the grim landscape with depressing gray concrete mounds and grassless dog runs seems better suited to the drug dealers and other assorted sleazes who are the main occupants. (Sara Krulwich/New York Times Photo) *LITE  (NYT8) NEW YORK -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- SCAPIN-THEATER-REVIEW-CLR, 1-09 -- Christopher Evan Welch, left, and Bill Irwin as a sly servant in his inspired new production of Moliere's ``Scapin,'' at the Laura Pels Theater at the Roundabout. Irwin is a prince of clowns with a distinctly nonearthly relationship to the basic laws of motion and anatomy: His very walk, a ballet of graceful awkwardness, suggests someone used to moving in another element. (Sara Krulwich/New York Times Photo)  (NYT7) NEW YORK -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- THEATER-NOTES-CLR, 1-09 -- Eric Schaeffer will direct ``Sweet Adeline'' in the ``Encores!'' series at City Center. The show, Jerome Kern and Oscar Hammerstein's follow-up to ``Show Boat,'' opened in 1929 and closed quickly, a casualty of the stock-market crash. It has been performed rarely, and usually with revisions. ``There's a lot of music, a lot of big numbers and a lot of choral work to be done, with only a week of rehearsal,'' he says. (Sara Krulwich/New York Times Photo) *LITE  (NYT6) NEW YORK -- Jan. 9, 1996 -- ADV. FOR SUN., JAN. 12 -- THEATER-DIRECTORS-CLR, 1-9 -- Anne Cattaneo, center, the head of the Director's Lab at the Lincoln Center Theater, sits surrounded by some of her charges. The lab is an attempt, says Ms. Cattaneo, ``to use the resources of this institution to give young directors access to the best directors we know.'' At the same time, she adds, ``we want to get as diverse a group as possible.'' (Frances Roberts/New York Times Photo)  (NYT5) BONN, Germany -- Jan. 9, 1996 -- ADV. FOR SUN., JAN. 12 -- EUROPE-THEATER-3-CLR, 1-9 -- ``I change my colors like a chameleon,'' says Leander Haussmann, who directs a municipal theater in Germany. ``I am interested in everything. I'm very proud of the variety we offer. Before I came here, it was very gray and very heavy. No one was having fun. I want people to relax and enjoy themselves.'' (Jonathan Player/New York Times Photo)  (NYT4) LONDON -- Jan. 9, 1996 -- ADV. FOR SUN., JAN. 12 -- EUROPE-THEATER-2-CLR, 1-9 -- Katie Mitchell in Camden Passage, a street in Islington, London, where she won the prestigious Evening Standard Award for directing last year. Last summer, reviewing her production of Euripides' ``Phoenician Women,'' Alastair Macauley wrote in The Financial Times that it was ``one of the finest productions of a Greek tragedy that I have ever seen, and one of the finest of all productions in recent RSC repertory.'' (Jonathan Player/New York Times Photo)  (NYT3) NANTERRE, France -- Jan. 9, 1996 -- ADV. FOR SUN., JAN. 12 -- EUROPE-THEATER-1-CLR, 1-9 -- Stanislas Nordey, associate artistic director the Theatre des Amandiers in the Paris suburb of Nanterre. Nordey is trying to revive theater's political idealism in a society that seems no longer interested in ideological debate. While he is praised for his stagecraft, Nordey said he picks his texts for their substance. ``They have to be powerfully poetic, but also have a clear political message,'' he explains. (Luc Perenom/New York Times Photo) *LITE  (NYT2) LOS ANGELES -- Jan. 9, 1996 -- ADV. FOR SUN., JAN. 12 -- KING-OF-THE-HILL-TV-CLR, 1-9 -- Sitting at an editing console, Greg Daniels, one of the writers of Fox's new animated show, ``King of the Hill.'' ``We tried to keep it realistically paced and about real people,'' he says. ``The show revolves around Hank Hill, a propane salesman who lives in a Texas suburb, and his relationships with his family, Bobby, his slow-witted son, his wife Peggy, who works as a substitute Spanish teacher, and his male buddies.'' (Ted Soqui/New York Times Photo and Fox Broadcasting Co.) *LITE  (NYT1) POTEET, Texas -- Jan. 9, 1996 -- ADV. FOR SUN., JAN. 12 -- SELENA-FILM-CLR, 1-9 -- Jennifer Lopez, during the filming of ``Selena, The Movie.'' Ms. Lopez is from the Castle Hill section of the Bronx, not South Texas, and is of Puerto Rican descent, not Mexican. ``But Selena was dark, like me, and had a Latin body, like mine, and didn't try to hide that,'' she says. Gregory Nava, the movie's Mexican-American director and screenwriter, describes Selena as ``the torchbearer for a new generation of Latinos.'' (Craig Stafford/New York Times Photo) *LITE  N.Y. (NYT24) NEW YORK -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- NY-MARIJUANA-CLUB-2-CLR, 1-09 -- A member of the New York City Medical Marijuana Buyers' Club uses the smell test to determine the marijuana's quality. Under New York State law, those who buy each week from the wholesale club are criminals. But the passage of referendums in California and Arizona legalizing marijuana for medical uses has caused the group's leaders to go more public, working to make a similar change in New York. (Michelle V. Agins/New York Times Photo)  (NYT23) NEW YORK -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- NY-MARIJUANA-CLUB-1-CLR, 1-09 -- Johann Moore, 34, a leader of the New York City Medical Marijuana Buyers' Club, is trying to raise the group's profile. ``They don't want to bust people who are sick and saving their lives, and would say that in court,'' he says of possible police intervention. His membership card to the club, which has existed for nearly four years. (Michelle V. Agins/New York Times Photo)  (NYT22) UNDATED -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- NY-WHITNEY-ESTATE, 1-09 -- Marylou Whitney, wife of Cornelius Vanderbilt Whitney, a millionaire industrialist. With timber prices down and property taxes on the rise, the family - headed by Mrs. Whitney since her husband's death at 92 in 1992 - proposed Thursday to subdivide and sell nearly a third of a 51,000 acre estate in the Adirondacks. (Bill Cunningham/New York Times Photo)  (NYT21) WASHINGTON -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- EXCLUSIVE-GINGRICH, 1-09 -- A tape of a telephone call between Speaker Newt Gingrich - shown greeting House members Thursday - and other Republicans shows him helping to orchestrate a response to charges by an ethics subcommittee. The call was taped by people in Florida who were unsympathetic to Gingrich and who heard it on a police scanner that picked up the cell phone transmissions. It was given to a Democratic congressman, who made the tape available to The New York Times. (Amy Toensing/New York Times Photo)  (NYT20) VARNER, Ark. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- ARK-EXECUTE-CLR, 1-09 -- Virginia Hamilton and David Small hug each other after Paul Ruiz and Earl Van Denton are executed in Arkansas on Wednesday. The two men shot Small and killed Mrs. Hamilton's father and six others in 1977. ``I hope I have some relief and the nightmares will stop,'' says Anne Jester, whose father, Opal James, a 58-year-old park ranger, was also killed by Van Denton and Ruiz after their escape from prison in Oklahoma. (Spencer Tirey/New York Times Photo)  (NYT19) JACKSONVILLE, Fla. -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- JAGUARS-OWNER-CLR, 1-09 -- Wayne Weaver, who once guided the growth of the Nine West shoe retailer, is leading the expansion Jacksonville Jaguars to success in the NFL. Weaver insists that the rules for operating an expansion football team are similar to those for running other companies: know your product and your consumer, hire smart people, and give them authority and support. (Oscar Sosa/New York Times Photo)  (NYT18) NANG RONG, Thailand -- Jan. 9, 1997 -- THAI-ENTREPENEUR, 1-09 -- Employees of the shoe factory in rural Nang Rong are just as busy as their counterparts in Bangkok, but outside work they have been able to recapture a more measured way of life. The factory here is one of several such enterprises created by Mechai Viravaidya, who has turned his energies toward the problem of rural poverty. ``It's reverse migration,'' he says. ``We have one factory where 80 percent of the people have come back from Bangkok.'' Times News Service  ALL 29 DIE IN PLANE CRASH OUTSIDE DETROIT  DETROIT (NYT) _ A twin-engine turboprop plane operated by Comair, a commuter air carrier affiliated with Delta Air Lines, crashed on Thursday afternoon in a snowstorm while approaching the Detroit Metropolitan Airport. All 26 passengers and 3 crew members are believed to have died.  The flight from Cincinnati went down in a field 18 miles southwest of the airport. The Federal Aviation Administration said that the pilot had been approaching the airport's Runway 3 Right and that he had not mentioned anything unusual to the air traffic controller before the crash.  The airport reported that at the time of the crash visibility at the airport was 1.5 miles with snow showers and several layers of clouds. But Doug Dandron, the communications supervisor at the dispatcher's office of the Monroe County Sheriff's Office, which responded to the crash, said there was ``very, very heavy snow.''  VOLKSWAGEN TO PAY GM $100 MILLION IN ESPIONAGE SUIT  DETROIT (NYT) _ Bringing to an end a four-year feud between two of the world's largest companies, Volkswagen AG agreed on Thursday to pay $100 million to General Motors Corp. to settle a lawsuit contending that VW stole trade secrets from GM.  In the settlement, one of the largest in a corporate espionage case, Volkswagen also agreed to buy at least $1 billion worth of auto parts from the American company over the next seven years. While not admitting any wrongdoing, VW said that it ``acknowledges the possibility that illegal activities'' by a team of executives who defected from GM to VW may have occurred.  While the agreement means that both companies can go back to building cars rather than legal cases, the central figure in the case, Jose Ignacio Lopez de Arriortua, still faces criminal charges in Germany. And the Justice Department is still looking into the matter.  DEMOCRATS CRITICIZE DELAY OF GINGRICH REPORT BY ETHICS PANEL  WASHINGTON (NYT) _ Democrats reacted angrily on Thursday to a decision to delay the release of a full ethics report against Speaker Newt Gingrich until after the House votes on his punishment and suggested that the Republican leader was orchestrating the investigation.  The Democrats demanded that the scheduled vote be postponed.  ``The Republican leadership is trying to bury the Gingrich ethics case by holding the House vote the day after the president is inaugurated, and by releasing the bipartisan ethics report on the day of the president's State of the Union Address,'' said Rep. David Bonior of Michigan, the Democratic whip and Gingrich's major political tormentor in the House.  A spokesman for the Republican National Campaign Committee called the Democrats' complaints a political smoke screen to drag out the ethics charges against Gingrich.  STUDY: RED WINE AND GRAPES MAY INHIBIT CANCER  (NYT) _ Grapes and red wine, already heralded for their potential to prevent heart disease, may also harbor a potent cancer inhibitor, according to a study being published Friday in the journal Science.  Researchers at the University of Illinois showed in preliminary laboratory and animal studies that the substance, resveratrol, which is prominent in the skin of grapes, may interfere with the development of cancer on several levels: by blocking the action of carcinogens, inhibiting the initiation and promotion of tumors and causing precancerous cells to revert to normal.  But it is not yet known whether eating grapes or drinking red wine results in biologically useful levels of resveratrol in a person's blood. Researchers have not demonstrated that resveratrol, which is soluble in alcohol but not water, is absorbed by humans who consume it through foods or wine.  HELMSLEY'S WILL LEAVES $1.7 BILLION TO HIS WIDOW  NEW YORK (NYT) _ Except for the $25,000 he left to his longtime secretary, Harry Helmsley bequeathed his entire $1.7 billion empire to his wife, Leona, automatically anointing her as one of the dominant forces in New York City real estate.  Considering the vastness of Helmsley's holdings, his will was a fairly straightforward 18-page document that was filed Thursday morning in Surrogate's Court in Manhattan. By just after noon, the will had been approved by Surrogate Eve Preminger.  The will transfers to Mrs. Helmsley ownership and management over Helmsley's stable of properties, from the Empire State Building and One Penn Plaza to a chain of hotels that includes the Helmsley Park Lane and the St. Moritz. It also gives her the freedom to transfer or sell any properties.  ``Now she's in an extraordinarily important position,'' said Howard Rubenstein, a spokesman for the family. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19970109.0724 type=advis > <DATELINE>  (BC-PHOTO-FULL-UPDATE-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> ATTENTION EDITORS: The following photos are part of tonight's N.Y. (NYT23) CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- March 6, 1997 -- HARVARD-RESIGN, 3-6 -- Albert Carnesale resigns as Harvard's provost to head the University of California at Los Angeles. Carnesale, a 60-year-old nuclear engineer and arms control expert, has been in Harvard's No. 2 job since 1994. ``I think this is a terrible loss for Harvard,'' says Marshall Goldman, the associate director of the Kathryne W. and Shelby Cullom Davis Center for Russian Studies. (Sara Krulwich/New York Times Photo)  (NYT16) NEW YORK -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV. FOR SUN., MARCH 9 -- ENGLISH-DANCING-CLR, 3-6 -- English country dancers following their lead at the Metropolitan-Duane United Methodist Church in the West Village. The first historical mention of English country dancing was during the reign of Queen Elizabeth I, who was reported to enjoy watching the ladies of the court perform ``country dances,'' simpler dances than the formal, balletic ones she herself was expected to dance. (Stephanie Berger/New York Times Photo)  (NYT15) UNDATED -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV. FOR SUN., MARCH 9 -- INVEST-COLLEGE-BONDS-CLR, 3-6 -- Sizing up colleges on the basis of bond ratings may yield surprises. Grinnell College of Iowa, left, has a AAA rating from Moody's Investors Service for its bonds. (Guido Cassetta/New York Times Photo). Despite its stellar academics, Georgetown University, right, only has an A-1 rating from Moody's, placing it in the middle of the investment-grade risk spectrum. (Steve Pope/New York Times Photo)  (NYT14) SCITUATE, Mass. -- March 6, 1997 -- BIKERS-RECORD-BOS-CLR, 3-6 -- Wayne Ross came home from the hospital following 17-weeks of treatment after being involved in an accident which left him a quadraplegic. Ross, 31, broke his neck when he crashed into the back of a bus halfway through a 16,000-mile bike trek from the top of Alaska to the tip of Argentina to raise funds for Multiple Sclerosis. (John Bohn/Boston Globe Photo) *LITE  (NYT13) UNDATED -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV. FOR SUN., MARCH 9 -- ABOUT-CARS-CADILLAC-2-CLR, 3-6 -- The Cadillac Catera has an edge over the Lexus ES300 in most interior dimensions except for front legroom. However, both the Cadillac and the Lexus can easily carry five passengers in their roomy, airy cabins. The Catera, top, lacks storage space, but its controls are neatly grouped in a central panel. The ES300, below, is plusher than ever, with loose-fitting leather seats and more wood trim. (Peter Yates/New York Times Photo)  (NYT12) UNDATED -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV. FOR SUN., MARCH 9 -- ABOUT-CARS-CADILLAC-1-CLR, 3-6 -- Cadillac has, at long last, introduced a smaller, sportier car aimed at the baby boomers - the Catera, right, a sport sedan built in Germany by Opel, GM's European unit. However, the best-seller in this $30,000 to $40,000 luxury car segment is the Japanese-made Lexus ES300, left. It has been remade for 1997 and is better than ever - and even less expensive than before. (Peter Yates/New York Times Photo)  (NYT11) BOSTON -- March 6, 1997 -- GARDEN-COLUMN-BOS-CLR, 3-6 -- From left, Jim Connor, chief horticulturalist for the City of Boston, Justine Liff, Boston Park Commissioner, and John Peterson, executive director of the Massachusetts Horticulture Society, inspect some begonias that the City of Boston will include in its recreation of the Public Garden during the upcoming New England Flower Show. (Pam Berry/Boston Globe Photo)  (NYT10) NEW YORK -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV. FOR SUN., MARCH 9 -- LOUISE-BOURGEOIS-ART, 3-6 -- Louise Bourgeoisin her Brooklyn appartment. At 85, Ms. Bourgeois is making her 19th appearance in the forthcoming Whitney Biennial show. ``My inspiration comes from childhood, the dysfunctional family,'' she says in a firm voice heavily accented with her native French, ``which was not my family, but my grandparents' and parents.'' (Sylvia Plachy/New YORk Times Photo)  (NYT9) NEW YORK -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV. FOR SUN., MARCH 9 -- FITZSIMMONS-COMIC, 3-6 -- Greg Fitzsimmons in Times Square. With ``Temporarily Fitzsimmons,'' a segment he does for ``USA Live'' in which he experiences life in a new workplace every week, and ``Idiot Savants,'' a quirky MTV quiz show for which he is the host, Fitzsimmons is currently New York comedy's golden boy. (Jean-Christian Bourcart/New York Times Photo) *LITE  (NYT8) NEW YORK -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV. FOR SUN., MARCH 9 -- OMER-AVITAL-JAZZ, 3-6 -- Omer Avital and his band perform at Smalls in New York City. With an idea of jazz that includes old and new definitions of swing and temperament, the musician, who is nearly 26, has been honing his band for the last 16 months. (Jean-Christian Bourcart/New York Times Photo)  (NYT7) SAN FRANCISCO -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV. FOR SUN., MARCH 9 -- AMERICANA-MUSIC-2-CLR, 3-6 -- Rob Bleetstein, editor of Americana, in his San Francisco office. About two-thirds of the stations playing Americana are commercial and one-third are public radio, Bleetstein says. The ones doing best, he adds, have cultivated small, loyal audiences in cities where a few big stations dominate the mainstream. (Peter DaSilva/New York Times Photo) *LITE  (NYT6) NEW YORK -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV. FOR SUN., MARCH 9 -- AMERICANA-MUSIC-1-CLR, 3-6 -- Singer and songwriter Chris Smither, performs on radio station WFUV at Fordam University. One of Smither's recent releases in number 5 on a chart that is tracking an emerging musical category called Americana. It includes acts ranging from veterans like Johnny Cash and Jerry Jeff Walker to quirky new groups like the Freight Hoppers and Bad Livers, a punk-bluegrass band.(Librado Romero/New York Times Photo)  (NYT5) NEW YORK -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV.
