Schulman, with choreography by Kathleen Marshall, ``Violet'' opens on Tuesday at Manhattan's Playwrights Horizons.(Nancy Siesel/New York Times Photo)  (NYT4) NEW YORK -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV. FOR SUN., MARCH 9 -- VIOLET-MUSICAL-1-CLR, 3-6 -- In ``Violet'' Lauren Ward plays the title role and Michael McElroy plays a Vietnam-bound soldier. ``Violet'' is the story of a severely disfigured young woman who journeys from North Carolina to Oklahoma in 1964 to find a preacher with healing hands who will make her beautiful.(Sara Krulwich/New York Times Photo)  (NYT3) CEDAR RAPIDS, Iowa -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV. FOR SUN., MARCH 9 -- CEDAR-RAPIDS-SYMPHONY-3-CLR, 3-6 -- Jeff Smith, 35, is the board president of the Cedar Rapids Symphony and president of midwestern division of Ryan Construction. When asked whether the orchestra's season might grow beyond its seven classical weekends, Smith says ``There is no reason for growth.'' (Mark Tade/New York Times Photo)  (NYT2) UNDATED -- March 6, 1997 -- ADV. FOR SUN., MARCH 9 -- CEDAR-RAPIDS-SYMPHONY-2-CLR, 3-6 -- The principal flutist, Jane Walker, top, is a 25-year member of the Cedar Rapids Symphony. She and her husband operate this music store specializing in woodwind and brass repertory in nearby Iowa City. Stephanie Wagor, below, runs the Symphony School she founded 10 years ago. She teaches music at the daycare center with the help of her son, Rich, a master student at the Manhattan School of Music in New York City. They are sitting in Cedar Rapids's Paramount Theater. (NYT23) CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- March 6, 1997 -- HARVARD-RESIGN, 3-6 -- Albert Carnesale resigns as Harvard's provost to head the University of California at Los Angeles. Carnesale, a 60-year-old nuclear engineer and arms control expert, has been in Harvard's No. 2 job since 1994. ``I think this is a terrible loss for Harvard,'' says Marshall Goldman, the associate director of the Kathryne W. and Shelby Cullom Davis Center for Russian Studies. (Kirsten Elstner/New York Times Photo)  (NYT22) NEW YORK -- March 6, 1997 -- BKC-NORTHEAST-CLR, 3-6 -- Richie Parker, whose free throws in the final seconds iced Long Island University's victory in the Northeast Conference championship game Thursday, begins a long-awaited celebration. As a result of the 72-67 victory over a gritty Monmouth team, LIU, earned its first NCAA appearance in 13 years. ``You could feel the intensity in the air,'' says Coach Ray Haskins. (Michelle V. Agins/New York Times Photo)  (NYT21) NEW YORK -- March 6, 1997 -- NY-TREE-ACCIDENT-2-CLR, 3-6 -- Rescue workers and police officers were at the site in Queens, Thursday where a 70-foot maple tree fell and crushed four girls to death in a van. Afterward, neighbors said the city had failed to respond to their concerns about the tree. City officials said that their records showed that someone had requested an inspection of the tree in June 1993, but that it was never inspected. (Steve Berman/New York Times Photo)  (NYT20) NEW YORK -- March 6, 1997 -- NY-TREE-ACCIDENT-1-CLR, 3-6 -- A 70-foot tree blown over by high winds smashed a school van, killing four schoolgirls, in Queens on Thursday. The driver, Maxine Capers, 61, shrieked ``Get the babies out! Get the babies out!'' as she crawled bleeding from the wreckage. Witnesses told of howling gusts and a roar like thunder as the great maple, 70 feet tall and 4 feet in diameter, came down. (Steve Berman/New York Times Photo)  (NYT19) WASHINGTON -- March 6, 1997 -- FUNDRAISING-CLR, 3-6 -- Senators John Glenn, left, and Fred Thompson appearing before the Rules Committee during its consideration of a budget for its inquiry on campaign fund-raising. (ATTENTION EDITORS: This briefs column is for use by clients of The New York Times Syndicate's SCIENTIFIC AMERICAN NEWS SERVICE. For information about becoming a client of this service, contact the New York Times Syndicate sales representative listed at the end of the text.) c.2002 Scientific American ALASKAN SHARK BOOM BLAMED IN PART ON GLOBAL WARMING By GREG MONE  As evidence for global warming accumulates, predictions concerning its effects are growing in scope and magnitude.  Two separate presentations made at the annual meeting of the American Association for the Advancement of Science demonstrated the range of global warming's possible consequences, from a boom in the shark population in Alaskan waters to a sharper rise in sea level than previously estimated.  According to Vince Gallucci of the University of Washington, the combined effects of a periodic swing in weather (called the Pacific Decadal Oscillation) and global warming may be making Alaska's Prince William Sound more hospitable to local sharks.  Meanwhile, Galucci notes, the number of pinnipeds, such as seals and sea lions, has decreased. In fact, Pacific sleeper sharks, which are capable of growing to 24 feet, and the much smaller salmon sharks have replaced the pinnipeds as the second-most efficient predators in the area, behind orcas.  Perhaps even more significant, the very waters these creatures swim in could rise much higher than recently predicted.  After analyzing the melting rate of glaciers on the West Coast of Alaska and northern Canada, Mark Meier and Mark Dyurgerov of the University of Colorado at Boulder have concluded that forecasts made last year by the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) failed to take into account an acceleration in glacial and ice cap melting that began in 1988. Since then, Meier says, the rate of ice loss has doubled.  He notes that the Alaskan and Canadian glaciers are important because they currently account for about half of the rate of global ice loss, yet they make up only about one-sixth of total glacial ice area.  In contrast to the IPCC's prediction that glacial melting will elevate sea levels by 0.16 to 0.36 feet, the new calculations indicate that the figure should be 0.65 feet or more.  Although this might not seem large, Meier notes that a 1-foot rise in sea level normally corresponds to a loss of 100 feet of beach. A rise of 1 meter, he adds, would flood half of Bangladesh, displacing 100 million people. A TASTE FOR PROTEIN By SARAH GRAHAM  Protein is an important part of a healthy diet. Now scientists have identified a taste receptor that may play a part in making sure we consume enough of it.  According to a report published online by the journal Nature, the receptor responds to the taste of amino acids, the building blocks of protein.  Charles Zuker of the University of California, San Diego and colleagues discovered and tested the receptor, dubbed T1R1plus3, in both mice and humans. In both species, the team found that the receptor responded to most of the 20 standard amino acids but did not react to similar natural and artificial compounds.  ``Our results,'' the authors write, ``demonstrate that T1R1 and T1R3 combine to function as a broadly tuned amino acid receptor.''  In humans, the receptor is more sensitive to the chemical glutamate than to any other amino acid. A related molecule, monosodium glutamate (MSG), is responsible for evoking the ``umami'' taste.  The researchers further determined that the genes for T1R1 and T1R3, which make up the new receptor, display individual variation. Such differences, they note, could account for subjective differences in the sense of taste. SAY GOODBYE TO FAST-FADING CELL-PHONE BATTERIES By GREG MONE  The days of fast-fading cellular phone batteries may soon be over.  Researchers at the Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory (LLNL) recently developed a working prototype for a portable fuel cell energy source that could power a cellular phone 300 percent longer than existing rechargeable batteries do. Indeed, the new technology would be less expensive, smaller and more powerful than any battery currently in use, according to Jeff Morse of LLNL's Center for Microtechnology Engineering.  He predicts that it could replace standard lithium-ion and lithium-ion polymer batteries in a number of consumer electronics products, such as laptops and handheld computers.  The new power source, which runs on liquid fuels, has at its core a thin layer of electrolyte materials sandwiched between electrode materials.  As control elements distribute the fuel over one electrode surface, the other receives air. Heating of the electrolyte-electrode layers stimulates the flow of protons from the fuel, sending them across the electrolyte layer to the air-breathing electrode. The protons then react with oxygen to generate electrical current.  Conveniently, recharging the power source requires only a simple switch of fuel cartridges.  The higher energy capacity of this miniature thin-film fuel cell battery ``will lead to further new classes of personal electronics, such as autonomous sensors and communication devices that are not currently possible with existing battery technologies,'' Morse says. ``This will facilitate the integration of voice, data and computing technologies that cannot be achieved with today's technologies.'' -0-  For more information on the topics covered in the Scientific American News Service, visit www.sciam.com. Since then, Meier says, the rate of ice loss has doubled.  He notes that the Alaskan and Canadian glaciers are important because they currently account for about half of the rate of global ice loss, yet they make up only about one-sixth of total glacial ice area.  In contrast to the IPCC's prediction that glacial melting will elevate sea levels by 0.16 to 0.36 feet, the new calculations indicate that the figure should be 0.65 feet or more.  Although this might not seem large, Meier notes that a 1-foot rise in sea level normally corresponds to a loss of 100 feet of beach. A rise of 1 meter, he adds, would flood half of Bangladesh, displacing 100 million people. A TASTE FOR PROTEIN By SARAH GRAHAM  Protein is an important part of a healthy diet. Now scientists have identified a taste receptor that may play a part in making sure we consume enough of it.  According to a report published online by the journal Nature, the receptor responds to the taste of amino acids, the building blocks of protein.  Charles Zuker of the University of California, San Diego and colleagues discovered and tested the receptor, dubbed T1R1plus3, in both mice and humans. In both species, the team found that the receptor responded to most of the 20 standard amino acids but did not react to similar natural and artificial compounds.  ``Our results,'' the authors write, ``demonstrate that T1R1 and T1R3 combine to function as a broadly tuned amino acid receptor.''  In humans, the receptor is more sensitive to the chemical glutamate than to any other amino acid. A related molecule, monosodium glutamate (MSG), is responsible for evoking the ``umami'' taste.  The researchers further determined that the genes for T1R1 and T1R3, which make up the new receptor, display individual variation. Such differences, they note, could account for subjective differences in the sense of taste. SAY GOODBYE TO FAST-FADING CELL-PHONE BATTERIES By GREG MONE  The days of fast-fading cellular phone batteries may soon be over.  Researchers at the Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory (LLNL) recently developed a working prototype for a portable fuel cell energy source that could power a cellular phone 300 percent longer than existing rechargeable batteries do. Indeed, the new technology would be less expensive, smaller and more powerful than any battery currently in use, according to Jeff Morse of LLNL's Center for Microtechnology Engineering.  He predicts that it could replace standard lithium-ion and lithium-ion polymer batteries in a number of consumer electronics products, such as laptops and handheld computers.  The new power source, which runs on liquid fuels, has at its core a thin layer of electrolyte materials sandwiched between electrode materials.  As control elements distribute the fuel over one electrode surface, the other receives air. Heating of the electrolyte-electrode layers stimulates the flow of protons from the fuel, sending them across the electrolyte layer to the air-breathing electrode. The protons then react with oxygen to generate electrical current.  Conveniently, recharging the power source requires only a simple switch of fuel cartridges.  The higher energy capacity of this miniature thin-film fuel cell battery ``will lead to further new classes of personal electronics, such as autonomous sensors and communication devices that are not currently possible with existing battery technologies,'' Morse says. ``This will facilitate the integration of voice, data and computing technologies that cannot be achieved with today's technologies.'' -0-  For more information on the topics covered in the Scientific American News Service, visit www.sciam.com. (ATTENTION EDITORS: This briefs column is for use by clients of The New York Times Syndicate's SCIENTIFIC AMERICAN NEWS SERVICE. For information about becoming a client of this service, contact the New York Times Syndicate sales representative listed at the end of the text.) c.2002 Scientific American ALASKAN SHARK BOOM BLAMED IN PART ON GLOBAL WARMING By GREG MONE  As evidence for global warming accumulates, predictions concerning its effects are growing in scope and magnitude.  Two separate presentations made at the annual meeting of the American Association for the Advancement of Science demonstrated the range of global warming's possible consequences, from a boom in the shark population in Alaskan waters to a sharper rise in sea level than previously estimated.  According to Vince Gallucci of the University of Washington, the combined effects of a periodic swing in weather (called the Pacific Decadal Oscillation) and global warming may be making Alaska's Prince William Sound more hospitable to local sharks.  Meanwhile, Galucci notes, the number of pinnipeds, such as seals and sea lions, has decreased. In fact, Pacific sleeper sharks, which are capable of growing to 24 feet, and the much smaller salmon sharks have replaced the pinnipeds as the second-most efficient predators in the area, behind orcas.  Perhaps even more significant, the very waters these creatures swim in could rise much higher than recently predicted.  After analyzing the melting rate of glaciers on the West Coast of Alaska and northern Canada, Mark Meier and Mark Dyurgerov of the University of Colorado at Boulder have concluded that forecasts made last year by the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) failed to take into account an acceleration in glacial and ice cap melting that began in 1988. Since then, Meier says, the rate of ice loss has doubled.  He notes that the Alaskan and Canadian glaciers are important because they currently account for about half of the rate of global ice loss, yet they make up only about one-sixth of total glacial ice area.  In contrast to the IPCC's prediction that glacial melting will elevate sea levels by 0.16 to 0.36 feet, the new calculations indicate that the figure should be 0.65 feet or more.  Although this might not seem large, Meier notes that a 1-foot rise in sea level normally corresponds to a loss of 100 feet of beach. A rise of 1 meter, he adds, would flood half of Bangladesh, displacing 100 million people. A TASTE FOR PROTEIN By SARAH GRAHAM  Protein is an important part of a healthy diet. Now scientists have identified a taste receptor that may play a part in making sure we consume enough of it. In humans, the receptor is more sensitive to the chemical glutamate than to any other amino acid. A related molecule, monosodium glutamate (MSG), is responsible for evoking the ``umami'' taste.  The researchers further determined that the genes for T1R1 and T1R3, which make up the new receptor, display individual variation. Such differences, they note, could account for subjective differences in the sense of taste. COMMENTARY: TRIUMPH OF THE PRODIGAL SON  MARTINEZ-LULA-COLUMN _ It is no surprise Luis Inacio Lula da Silva of the leftist Workers Party won 38 million votes in Brazil's recent presidential election and is favored to win the runoff on Oct. 27, writes columnist Tomas Eloy Martinez. ``The general feeling in Brazil is that Lula's the only one who can pry the country from the vise of the IMF and foreign investors (and win some relief for its $240 billion debt).'' More importantly, he adds, it also reflects a growing disenchantment with the United States felt throughout Latin America, a region that had been courted so prettily by George W. Bush _ before Sept. 11, 2001.  (Tomas Eloy Martinez is the author of ``The Peron Novel,'' ``Santa Evita,'' and ``The Flight of the Queen'' which recently won Spain's most prestigious award for fiction. His books have been translated into more than 30 languages. Now there's a do-it-yourself test to see if your drink has been spiked with the date-rape drugs GHB or ketamine _ odorless, colorless sedatives that impair judgment and induce sleep.  The test, made by Drink Safe Technologies in Plantation, Fla., consists of a four-inch cardboard coaster that fits into a purse or pocket. Students can slip it under their beverage and dribble liquid with a swizzle stick or finger on one of two test areas. If the spot turns blue, the drink has been dosed. The company also makes a personal test kit with 20 strips.  The products will be in supermarkets and drug stores early this year, but more than 40 colleges and universities have already ordered them, including the University of Miami and Loyola University in New Orleans. St. Lawrence University in Canton, N.Y., has bought 600 coasters to distribute in the spring.  ``We're working toward developing a proactive and educational approach to distributing the cards,'' said Bettina Straight, assistant director of residential learning communities at St. Lawrence. ``We want them to be used appropriately as an added safety precaution and want to arm our students with information about what to do if they find their drink is contaminated, before or after they have actually ingested some of it.''  Stephen M. Thompson, sexual assault services coordinator at Central Michigan University in Mount Pleasant, is skeptical. For one, the coaster announces its intent: there's a big Drink Safe logo in the center that is designed, its makers say, to scare off potential assailants. But most people who use drugs to subdue a victim are known to their target, Thompson says, ``and the target feels fairly comfortable drinking with them.''  And, he adds, ``most people are not dosed in bars but at private parties. Realistically, at a party, are you going to have these things with you and test your drink with them? The likelihood is you are not.''  Dillydallying: Bad For Your Health  Here's some bad news for students who put off studying: procrastinators get more cold and flu symptoms and have more digestive problems than their punctual classmates.  They also tend toward an unhealthy lifestyle, according to a recent study of 374 undergraduates by the Procrastination Research Group at Carleton University in Ottawa. Student procrastinators are more likely to eat poorly and smoke, and they sleep less and drink more than students who do their homework promptly.  At the root of the problem is an inability to regulate behavior and control impulses _ say, drinking more than you had intended when you sat down at the bar. ``If you're quite impulsive then you're unable to protect one intention from another,'' says Timothy A. Pychyl, a psychologist and co-author of the study. Things can get worse when tasks are impersonal or out of one's control. ``Most assignments are not things that students initiated themselves,'' he says. ``They can lack meaning for that reason.''  Giving a procrastinator a hand-held organizer probably won't change habits in the long term. ``It's not about time management,'' Pychyl says. ``Some people will buy a day planner, fill it in and say that's it for today. It becomes part of the procrastination itself.''  Such dawdling is not unusual. In one survey, 70 percent of the students confessed to academic tardiness. Some favorite excuses: computer failure, leaving a paper at home and the death of a grandmother.  Is That Andromeda or a Truck Stop on the Turnpike? Finally, Focus  When the astronomer Ptolemy stared into the night sky to ``search out the massed wheeling circles of the stars,'' he felt as if he were ``side by side with Zeus himself'' taking in his ``fill of ambrosia, the food of the gods.''  Here's how Prof. Fred Chromey of Vassar College describes gazing at the stars from the typical campus observatory: ``You'd go into this really cold, dark room, clamp your eye to the eyepiece and after a long time of shifting focus, you'd see a _ fuzzy patch!''  Astronomy students long toiled in crumbling, moldy observatories, most built in the early 19th century in the darkest (i.e., most isolated) parts of campus to avoid light pollution. But in the mid-'90s, the cost of video and digital electronics plummeted, while the quality of results improved. With the technology that enabled the camcorder, it became possible to get brighter, crisper images and automatically pinpoint a position. Today's astronomers can view images and data on a computer screen from the comfort of the ``warm room,'' where they used to go to escape the chill of the open-domed observatory.  ``Now a small college can afford $20K and do research that gets you scientific results,'' Chromey said. ``It's a big difference for the students.'' Vassar overhauled its observatory in 1998.  Other campuses have been updating as well. A new observatory at the University of North Texas was completed in April. Eventually, students will be able to operate telescopes 45 miles away from campus via remote control. A Civil War-era observatory at Drake University in Des Moines is considered haunted (a university president and his wife are entombed behind a wall in the lobby). With water damage, a dented bronze dome and warped shutters, it looked the part. A rejuvenated observatory reopened in March.  Alfred University in upstate New York has spiffed up, too. ``We are now able to see objects you used to have the biggest telescopes on Earth to see,'' said G. David Toot, director of the Stull Observatory at Alfred. ``The whole experience is much friendlier. Now telescopes point themselves'' _ after being told, via computer, what to look at. ``Before we put in an automation system, finding some of these more distant targets could take easily 45 minutes to an hour. Now we have it in five minutes.''  Astronomy may be one of the more popular ways to fulfill a science requirement _ in 1999-2000, 163,000 students took an introductory course _ but some things haven't changed. Stargazing is a lonely field: only 202 bachelor's degrees and 199 doctorates in astronomy and astrophysics were conferred in 2000, according to the American Institute of Physics.  ``There always has been a relatively small number of people who are willing to study objects that are very remote and don't have direct application to everyday life,'' said Kevin B. Marvel, deputy executive officer of the 6,000-member American Astronomical Society. ``The pay is generally low and the hours are long and at night, and it requires 10 or 15 years of education in math and science and physics.''  ``Everybody loves the sky,'' he conceded, ``but you have to be devoted to study it for a living.''  Battle of the Campus TV Networks  The rocker Lisa Loeb bakes a banana cream pie; cameras trail the world's shortest porn star as she starts a new life; college kids are voted off a deserted island. This is college network television, where students get their own versions of ``Emeril Live,'' ``The Osbournes'' and ``Survivor,'' and advertisers get access to a desirable, often sequestered demographic.  In a bid to reach coveted 18- to 21-year-olds _ and to fill empty time slots on college stations _ major enterprises like MTV and National Lampoon have entered the college-only marketplace, wherein programming is piped solely into dorm rooms and dining halls via satellite or university cable systems.  ``We're going to really have our laser focus on this audience,'' says Donald Silvey, a programming executive at MTV. ``Everything we do is going to be programmed specifically for them.''  NATIONAL LAMPOON NETWORK: The idea of a national network aimed at college students was hatched in 1995 by five recent graduates sharing a ski vacation. They named it Burly Bear, and Lorne Michaels of ``Saturday Night Live'' became a major backer. National Lampoon bought the struggling network last fall.  Niche: National Lampoon, whose Ivy League pedigree includes producing ``Animal House,'' plans to add its own stamp to Burly Bear's irreverent reality programs and twisted interpretations of mainstream television shows.  Where: 420 campuses, including Duke, MIT and Brown.  What's on: ``Bridget the Midget'' follows the life of a three-foot-tall former porn star who is now an aspiring rock singer. Burly Bear holdovers include ``Half Baked,'' an anti-Martha Stewart cooking show that features celebrities like Shaquille O'Neal and Lisa Loeb sharing favorite, if unappetizing, recipes, and ``Gamers,'' in which two video- game wonks review new releases and crack jokes a la ``Beavis and Butthead.''  COLLEGE TELEVISION NETWORK: Ten years ago, an Atlanta company, Laser Video Network, began installing music video kiosks on campuses. In 1997, it changed its name to CTN and started beaming videos, news, sports and original shows to college television sets via satellite. Now the largest of the campus networks, CTN skips the dorm rooms favored by its competitors and offers 24/7 programming in common areas like dining halls and fitness centers. MTV bought the network this fall.  Niche: MTV for the all-nighter crowd. Executives say they will weave their music programming muscle into the current mix.  Where: 720 campuses, including Fordham, Michigan State and UCLA.  What's on: ``Pay Dirt,'' a job-search series, goes on location to the cubicles and laboratories of entry-level workers (one episode walked viewers through the making of a Twix Bars commercial). ``Backstage Pass'' features behind-the-microphone chats with college music favorites like John Mayer and Savage Garden.  ZILO NETWORKS: Begun in 2000 by two pay-per-view executives, Zilo cross-pollinates original programs with Web activity and live campus events.  Niche: X-Games edginess meets prime time. Extreme sports, emerging music and college-centric versions of reality shows.  Where: 256 campuses, including New York University, University of Southern California and Rutgers.  What's on: In ``Exiled,'' a ``Survivor'' takeoff, nine students swat bugs and compete for scholarship money. In ``Get Stupid,'' students agree to eat cat food, dance naked on dorm roofs and accept other dares for cash.   </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20030107.0232 type=story > <HEADLINE> AFTER ATTACK, CHIRAC EMPHASIZES IMPORTANCE OF FRENCH PEACEKEEPING </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> PARIS  (BC-IVORY-COAST-FRANCE-NY) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200301.gz"

The ring she is referring to is the cycle of plays that Wilson has written -- from Ma Rainey's Black Bottom in 1984 to his current offering, Gem of the Ocean -- depicting the African-American experience through each decade of the 20th century. While creating the lives of black families onstage, though, Wilson has succeeded in creating another family -- made up of the remarkable corps of actors, largely African-American, who have repeatedly filled roles small and large in his plays. It is a list that includes such mainstream stars as Angela Bassett, Whoopi Goldberg, Phylicia Rashad and Laurence Fishburne, as well as theater veterans like Anthony Chisholm. Hamilton, who ended up going to some school called Juilliard after Yale rejected her, eventually landed a small role in Wilson's 1989 play, Piano Lesson, and is now back on Broadway in Gem. I feel like I'm part of this very dignified royal family, she now says. By Jesse McKinley. </P> 	nyt_eng_200412.gz

The building has been photographed only a jillion times.  She thought the photos would help with her final paper in a State University of New York at Albany history class -- Albany, the City and its Architecture. But as Miner sat in her car taking pictures last week, parked between the Capitol and Empire State Plaza, somebody walked by and got the idea she was a threat. The person dialed 911 and gave the Albany Police Department her license plate number.  At 7 a.m. the next day, Miner got a call from her upset mother, Carol, in Rhode Island. The police had called Carol Miner to find out what her daughter, a senior planning on going to law school, was doing with a camera alongside New York's Capitol.  The police officer was really nice, said Ashley Miner, who said the cop was just doing his job.  Carol Miner was worried because she figured a call from the authorities so early in the morning could only mean bad news.  They said, 'This is the Albany police,' and my heart just automatically stopped, she said.  The officer questioned Carol Miner for about five minutes before being satisfied that Ashley was, indeed, just a student. She's never been in trouble, Carol Miner said proudly.  Detective James Miller, a spokesman for the city's Public Safety Department, said the officer's response to the 911 tip was typical. Any slight inconvenience, he said, is outweighed by the ultimate safety of the community.  Ashley Miner wound up calling the officer to explain herself. She said the officer told her the 911 caller believed Ashley was staring at him and looked suspicious.  She said she knew right away who the officer was talking about.  He stopped in front of my car and was staring at me -- that's why I left, she said.  Miner couldn't muster up anger at the conspiracy theorist, either. Because if something had happened, people would be praising him, she said.  What's sad, she said, is that we've created a society where we're that paranoid.  Miner squeezed just three frames before she drove off.  None of them came out.  But her professor, Warren Roberts, still gave her an A on the paper.  --  (Kenneth Aaron can be reached at aaronktimesunion.com) </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20041222.0078 type=story > <HEADLINE> MANY EASTERN UKRAINIANS FEEL FRUSTRATION AS REVOTE NEARS </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> DONETSK, Ukraine </DATELINE> <TEXT> 	nyt_eng_200412.gz

IRAQ (Baghdad, Iraq) -- American troops and armored vehicles clamped down on Mosul on Wednesday, a day after an attack on a nearby base in which 22 people were killed -- almost all of them Americans. The military said a suicide bomber probably had carried out the blast, the deadliest single attack on American troops since the war began.the war began.  IRAQ-SECURITY (Washington) -- The U.S. ground commander in Iraq on Wednesday acknowledged that insurgents had a good idea where and when large numbers of American troops in Mosul were assembled for meals, but defended the military's measures to protect the troops. Investigators were still sifting through clues to explain Tuesday's attack on a mess hall in Mosul that killed at least 22 people and appeared to have been the work of a suicide bomber. The chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, Gen. Richard B. Myers, and Defense Secretary Donald H. Rumsfeld, at a news conference, repeatedly said that it would continue to be difficult to protect against a determined insurgency, and that it would ultimately be necessary for Iraqi forces to achieve security for their own country. The enemy's got a brain, the enemy alters its tactics, Rumsfeld said. The intimidation brought on by violent attacks, he said, can prevent ordinary Iraqis from providing intelligence, running for office or voting. But they and other military leaders said there are no easy answers when it comes to protecting American troops, especially if they are perceived as an occupying force. By Eric Schmitt.  SAUDI-LIBYA (Cairo, Egypt) -- Prince Saud al-Faisal, the Saudi foreign minister, announced on Wednesday that the kingdom is expelling the Libyan ambassador and withdrawing its own envoy from Tripoli because of a Libyan plot to assassinate the crown prince. Speaking at a news conference in Riyadh, Prince Saud said his country was not breaking off relations, but was taking what he called limited measures despite the ugliness of what happened. Prince Saud said that the Libyan embassy in Riyadh and the Saudi embassy in Tripoli would remain open. He stressed that he did not want the Libyan people to suffer, particularly with the annual Muslim pilgrimage to Mecca and other holy sites starting in January. By Neil MacFarquhar.  MIDEAST (Jerusalem) -- The visiting British prime minister, Tony Blair, said on Wednesday that it was an opportune moment to relaunch Mideast peace efforts, but warned that the Palestinians needed to act against terrorism. Amid the improved regional climate, Blair plans to hold an international conference in Britain early next year that will encourage Palestinian political, economic and security reforms, though Israel will not be participating. By Greg Myre.  WASHINGTON  (Will move in w news file.)  ENVIRON-FORESTS (Washington) -- The Forest Service issues a new set of regulations loosening controls on how 191 million acres of national wilderness can be used. By Felicity Barringer.  NATIONAL  (Will move in a news file.)  WASH-ELECT (Seattle) -- The head of the Washington State Democratic Party says that recount results from King County give the Democrat, Christine Gregoire, an eight-vote victory in the closest governor's race in state history. The new tally will hardly resolves the matter, as the two sides prepare to go before the state Supreme Court to argue that 750 uncounted ballots found at the last minute should be included in the recount. By Sarah Kershaw.  GITMO-ABUSE (Washington) -- Internal FBI documents offer an unusual glimpse inside the bureau as it shifted into overdrive, after the first accusations of prisoner abuse in Iraq surfaced in May, to find out whether agents in Iraq and Guantanamo Bay, Cuba, were involved in coercive interrogations. The documents, which show the FBI struggling to define the law enforcement agency's role in intelligence interrogations, have been disclosed in recent weeks by the ACLU, which obtained them through a Freedom of Information Act request. "150607"	"he"	"adds"	" Waltz spells out his theory most thoroughly in the 1995 book The Spread of Nuclear Weapons, co-written with the Stanford political scientist Scott D. Sagan in the form of an extended debate. Updated and republished two years ago to take into account the nuclearization of India and Pakistan, it contains the same arguments Waltz makes today in interviews. Put simply, a war between nuclear powers cannot be decisively won without the risk of total destruction. Since the risk of escalation in any conflict is so high, nuclear states grow cautious. If states can score only small gains because large ones risk retaliation, Waltz writes, they have little incentive to fight. When fighting does break out, it is likely to be a localized proxy conflict like the Korean War instead of, say, a Soviet invasion of Western Europe. Nuclear weapons, he adds, even blunt the urge for territorial expansion, since they contribute far more to a country's security than any geographical buffer could. </P> "	"nyt_eng_200503.gz"
